style="font-size: 1rem;"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%90%AC%E9%87%8C%E9%95%B7%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:萬里長城">萬里長城</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Simplified Chinese</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans" style="font-size: 1rem;"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%87%E9%87%8C%E9%95%BF%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:万里长城">万里长城</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;">Literal meaning</th><td class="infobox-data">"The 10,000-<i><a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">li</a></i> Long Wall"</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" Chángchéng</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Wu Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="wuu-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Vae<sup>去</sup>-li<sup>上</sup> zan<sup>平</sup>-zen<sup>平</sup></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Yue: Cantonese</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Yale Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Maan<sup>6</sup>lei<sup>5</sup> Cheung<sup>4</sup>sing<sup>4</sup></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Maan<sup>6</sup>-lei<sup>5</sup> coeng<sup>4</sup>-sing<sup>4</sup></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">IPA</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal"><span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[màːn.le̬i tsʰœ̏ːŋ.sȅŋ]</a></span></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Romanization_System" title="Taiwanese Romanization System">Tâi-lô</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="nan-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Bān-lí tn̂g-siânn</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Great Wall of China</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">萬里長城</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">万里长城</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Wànlǐ Chángchéng</span></i>) is a series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortifications" title="Fortifications">fortifications</a> that were built across the historical northern borders of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_states" title="Ancient Chinese states">ancient Chinese states</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_China" title="Imperial China">Imperial China</a> as protection against <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_nomads" title="Eurasian nomads">various nomadic groups</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">Eurasian Steppe</a>. Several walls were built from as early as the 7th century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-4">[4]</a></sup> with selective stretches later joined together by <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> (220–206 BC), the first <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">emperor of China</a>. Little of the Qin wall remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brit_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brit-5">[5]</a></sup> Later on, many successive dynasties built and maintained multiple stretches of border walls. The best-known sections of the wall were built by the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> (1368–1644).
</p><p>Apart from defense, other purposes of the Great Wall have included <a href="/wiki/Border_control" title="Border control">border controls</a>, allowing the imposition of duties on goods transported along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, regulation or encouragement of trade and the control of immigration and emigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Furthermore, the defensive characteristics of the Great Wall were enhanced by the construction of <a href="/wiki/Watchtower" title="Watchtower">watchtowers</a>, troop barracks, garrison stations, signaling capabilities through the means of smoke or fire, and the fact that the path of the Great Wall also served as a transportation corridor.
</p><p>The frontier walls built by different dynasties have multiple courses. Collectively, they stretch from <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong</a> in the east to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lop_Lake" title="Lop Lake">Lop Lake</a> in the west, from the present-day <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Russia_border" title="China–Russia border">Sino–Russian border</a> in the north to <a href="/wiki/Tao_River" title="Tao River">Tao River</a> (Taohe) in the south; along an arc that roughly delineates the edge of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongolian_steppe" title="Mongolian steppe">Mongolian steppe</a>; spanning 21,196.18 km (13,170.70 mi) in total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Wall_of_China_CBC_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Wall_of_China_CBC-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncha_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncha-3">[3]</a></sup> Today, the defensive system of the Great Wall is generally recognized as one of the most impressive architectural feats in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Wall_of_China_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Wall_of_China-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="1709" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg/220px-%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg/330px-%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg/440px-%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E8%8F%AF%E5%A4%B7%E5%9C%96%E9%99%84%E6%B3%A8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Huayi_tu" title="Huayi tu">Huayi tu</a></i>, a 1136 map of China with the Great Wall depicted on the northern edge of the country</div></div></div>
<p>The collection of fortifications known as the Great Wall of China has historically had a number of different names in both Chinese and English.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">Chinese histories</a>, the term "Long Wall(s)" (<span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> 長城,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> 长城,</span> <i>Chángchéng</i>) appears in <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i>, where it referred both to the separate great walls built between and north of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> and to the more unified construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Emperor" title="First Emperor">First Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983650_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983650-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese character</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:城">城</a></span></span>, meaning city or fortress, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phono-semantic_compound" title="Phono-semantic compound">phono-semantic compound</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_radical" title="Earth radical">"earth" radical</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%9F" title="wikt:土">土</a></span></span> and phonetic <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%88%90" title="wikt:成">成</a></span></span>, whose <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> pronunciation has been <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">reconstructed</a> as *<i>deŋ</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It originally referred to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_city_wall" title="Chinese city wall">rampart</a> which surrounded <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_urban_planning" title="Ancient Chinese urban planning">traditional Chinese cities</a> and was used by extension for these walls around <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_states" title="Ancient Chinese states">their respective states</a>; today, however, it is much more often the Chinese word for "city".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The longer Chinese name "Ten-Thousand Mile Long Wall" (<span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> 萬里長城,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> 万里长城,</span> <i>Wànlǐ Chángchéng</i>) came from Sima Qian's description of it in the <i>Records</i>, though he did not name the walls as such. The AD 493 <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Song" title="Book of Song">Book of Song</a></i> quotes the frontier general <a href="/wiki/Tan_Daoji" title="Tan Daoji">Tan Daoji</a> referring to "the long wall of 10,000 miles", closer to the modern name, but the name rarely features in pre-modern times otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990202_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990202-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_mile" title="Traditional Chinese mile">traditional Chinese mile</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%8C" title="wikt:里">里</a></span></span>, <i>lǐ</i>) was an often irregular distance that was intended to show the length of a standard village and varied with terrain but was usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_units" title="Traditional Chinese units">standardized</a> at distances around a third of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_mile" title="English mile">English mile</a> (540 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winborn1994_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winborn1994-13">[13]</a></sup>  However, this use of "ten-thousand" (<i>wàn</i>) is <a href="/wiki/Literal_and_figurative_language" title="Literal and figurative language">figurative</a> in a similar manner to the Greek and English <i><a href="/wiki/Myriad" title="Myriad">myriad</a></i> and simply means "innumerable" or "immeasurable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindy_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindy-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the wall's association with the First Emperor's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_Shihuang#Historiography" title="Qin Shihuang">supposed tyranny</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_dynasties" title="List of Chinese dynasties">Chinese dynasties</a> after <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin</a> usually avoided referring to their own additions to the wall by the name "Long Wall".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983651_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983651-15">[15]</a></sup> Instead, various terms were used in medieval records, including "frontier(s)" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A1%9E" title="wikt:塞">塞</a></span></span>, <i>Sài</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELovell200615_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovell200615-16">[16]</a></sup> "rampart(s)" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9E%A3" title="wikt:垣">垣</a></span></span>, <i>Yuán</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELovell200615_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovell200615-16">[16]</a></sup> "barrier(s)" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9A%9C" title="wikt:障">障</a></span></span>, <i>Zhàng</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELovell200615_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovell200615-16">[16]</a></sup> "the outer fortresses" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%96" title="wikt:外">外</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A0%A1" title="wikt:堡">堡</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Wàibǎo</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron199049_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron199049-17">[17]</a></sup> and "the border wall(s)" <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%82%8A" title="wikt:邊">邊</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%89%86" title="wikt:牆">牆</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%BE%B9" title="wikt:边">边</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A2%99" title="wikt:墙">墙</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Biānqiáng</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983651_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983651-15">[15]</a></sup> Poetic and informal names for the wall included "the Purple Frontier" <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%B4%AB" title="wikt:紫">紫</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A1%9E" title="wikt:塞">塞</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Zǐsài</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron199021_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron199021-18">[18]</a></sup> and "the Earth Dragon" <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%9F" title="wikt:土">土</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BE%8D" title="wikt:龍">龍</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%9F" title="wikt:土">土</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BE%99" title="wikt:龙">龙</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Tǔlóng</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron198869_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron198869-19">[19]</a></sup> Only during the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing period</a> did "Long Wall" become the catch-all term to refer to the many border walls regardless of their location or dynastic origin, equivalent to the English "Great Wall".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHessler200759_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHessler200759-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sections of the wall in south <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolian</a> <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> are sometimes referred to as "Wall of Genghis Khan", even though Genghis Khan did not construct any walls or permanent defense lines himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current English name evolved from accounts of <span class="nowrap">"the Chinese wall"</span> from early modern European travelers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHessler200759_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHessler200759-20">[20]</a></sup> By the nineteenth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHessler200759_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHessler200759-20">[20]</a></sup> "the Great Wall of China" had become standard in English and French, although other European languages such as German continue to refer to it as "the Chinese wall".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindy_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-lindy-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China" title="History of the Great Wall of China">History of the Great Wall of China</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_walls">Early walls</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_Qi" title="Great Wall of Qi">Great Wall of Qi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreatWallofQinDynasty.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="379" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/GreatWallofQinDynasty.png/220px-GreatWallofQinDynasty.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/GreatWallofQinDynasty.png/330px-GreatWallofQinDynasty.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/GreatWallofQinDynasty.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GreatWallofQinDynasty.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Wall of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin</a> stretches from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lintao" title="Lintao">Lintao</a> to Liaodong</div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese were already familiar with the techniques of <a href="/wiki/Wall" title="Wall">wall-building</a> by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> between the 8th and 5th centuries BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> During this time and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, the states of <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wei_(state)" title="Wei (state)">Wei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(state)" title="Zhao (state)">Zhao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qi_(state)" title="Qi (state)">Qi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Han_(state)" title="Han (state)">Han</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yan_(state)" title="Yan (state)">Yan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zhongshan_(state)" title="Zhongshan (state)">Zhongshan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> all constructed extensive fortifications to defend their own borders. Built to withstand the attack of small arms such as swords and spears, these walls were made mostly of stone or by stamping earth and gravel between board frames.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="524" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png/220px-GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png/330px-GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png/440px-GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GreatWallOfHanDynasty.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Wall of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> is the longest of all walls, from Mamitu near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yumenguan" title="Yumenguan">Yumenguan</a> to Liaodong</div></div></div>
<p>King Zheng of <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a> conquered the last of his opponents and unified China as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Emperor" title="First Emperor">First Emperor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> ("Qin Shi Huang") in 221 BC.  Intending to impose centralized rule and prevent the resurgence of feudal lords, he ordered the destruction of the sections of the walls that divided his empire among the former states. To position the empire against the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> people from the north, however, he ordered the building of new walls to connect the remaining fortifications along the empire's northern frontier. "Build and move on" was a central guiding principle in constructing the wall, implying that the Chinese were not erecting a permanently fixed border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transporting the large quantity of materials required for construction was difficult, so builders always tried to use local resources. Stones from the mountains were used over mountain ranges, while <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> was used for construction in the plains. There are no surviving historical records indicating the exact length and course of the Qin walls. Most of the ancient walls have eroded away over the centuries, and very few sections remain today. The human cost of the construction is unknown, but it has been estimated by some authors that hundreds of thousands<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlavicekMitchellMatray200535_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlavicekMitchellMatray200535-26">[26]</a></sup> workers died building the Qin wall. Later, the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mongolia_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-mongolia-27">[27]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_dynasties" title="Northern dynasties">Northern dynasties</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a> all repaired, rebuilt, or expanded sections of the Great Wall at great cost to defend themselves against northern invaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983653_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983653-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> dynasties did not undertake any significant effort in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983653_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983653-28">[28]</a></sup> Dynasties founded by non-Han ethnic groups also built their border walls: the <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a>-ruled <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitan</a>-ruled <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tangut_people" title="Tangut people">Tangut</a>-established <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a>, who ruled vast territories over Northern China throughout centuries, all constructed defensive walls but those were located much to the north of the other Great Walls as we know it, within China's autonomous region of <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> and in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983654Haw200652–54_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1983654Haw200652–54-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ming_era">Ming era</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ming_Great_Wall" title="Ming Great Wall">Ming Great Wall</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreatWallChina4.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="934" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/GreatWallChina4.png/220px-GreatWallChina4.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/GreatWallChina4.png/330px-GreatWallChina4.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/GreatWallChina4.png/440px-GreatWallChina4.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GreatWallChina4.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The extent of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming Empire</a> and its walls</div></div></div>
<p>The Great Wall concept was revived again under the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> in the 14th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarnow2008192_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarnow2008192-30">[30]</a></sup> and following the Ming army's defeat by the <a href="/wiki/Oirats" title="Oirats">Oirats</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tumu" title="Battle of Tumu">Battle of Tumu</a>. The Ming had failed to gain a clear upper hand over the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a> tribes after successive battles, and the long-drawn conflict was taking a toll on the empire. The Ming adopted a new strategy to keep the nomadic tribes out by constructing walls along the northern border of China. Acknowledging the Mongol control established in the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_Desert" title="Ordos Desert">Ordos Desert</a>, the wall followed the desert's southern edge instead of incorporating the bend of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>.
</p><p>Unlike the earlier fortifications, the Ming construction was stronger and more elaborate due to the use of bricks and stone instead of rammed earth. Up to 25,000 watchtowers are estimated to have been constructed on the wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESzabóDávidLoczy2010220_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESzabóDávidLoczy2010220-31">[31]</a></sup> As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions" title="Mongol invasions">Mongol raids</a> continued periodically over the years, the Ming devoted considerable resources to repair and reinforce the walls. Sections near the Ming capital of Beijing were especially strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2006177_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2006177-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Qi_Jiguang" title="Qi Jiguang">Qi Jiguang</a> between 1567 and 1570 also repaired and reinforced the wall, faced sections of the ram-earth wall with bricks and constructed 1,200 watchtowers from Shanhaiguan Pass to Changping to warn of approaching Mongol raiders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> During the 1440s–1460s, the Ming also built a so-called "Liaodong Wall". Similar in function to the Great Wall (whose extension, in a sense, it was), but more basic in construction, the Liaodong Wall enclosed the agricultural heartland of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liaodong" title="Liaodong">Liaodong</a> province, protecting it against potential incursions by Jurchen-Mongol Oriyanghan from the northwest and the <a href="/wiki/Jianzhou_Jurchens" title="Jianzhou Jurchens">Jianzhou Jurchens</a> from the north. While stones and tiles were used in some parts of the Liaodong Wall, most of it was in fact simply an earth dike with moats on both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds198538–40_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds198538–40-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Towards the end of the Ming, the Great Wall helped defend the empire against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">Manchu</a> invasions that began around 1600. Even after the loss of all of <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">Liaodong</a>, the Ming army held the heavily fortified <a href="/wiki/Shanhai_Pass" title="Shanhai Pass">Shanhai Pass</a>, preventing the Manchus from conquering the Chinese heartland. The Manchus were finally able to cross the Great Wall in 1644, after Beijing had already fallen to <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">Li Zicheng</a>'s short-lived <a href="/wiki/Shun_dynasty" title="Shun dynasty">Shun dynasty</a>. Before this time, the Manchus had crossed the Great Wall multiple times to raid, but this time it was for conquest. The gates at Shanhai Pass were opened on May 25 by the commanding Ming general, <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">Wu Sangui</a>, who formed an alliance with the Manchus, hoping to use the Manchus to expel the rebels from Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELovell2006254_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovell2006254-35">[35]</a></sup> The Manchus quickly seized Beijing, and eventually defeated both the Shun dynasty and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming" title="Southern Ming">remaining Ming resistance</a>, consolidating the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> over all of <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott20011–2_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott20011–2-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Qing rule, China's borders extended beyond the walls and <a href="/wiki/Dzungar%E2%80%93Qing_Wars" title="Dzungar–Qing Wars">Mongolia was annexed</a> into the empire, so constructions on the Great Wall were discontinued. On the other hand, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Willow_Palisade" title="Willow Palisade">Willow Palisade</a>, following a line similar to that of the Ming Liaodong Wall, was constructed by the Qing rulers in Manchuria. Its purpose, however, was not defense but rather to prevent Han Chinese migration into Manchuria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_accounts">Foreign accounts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_(April_1853,_X,_p.41)_-_Copy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="999" data-file-width="982" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_%28April_1853%2C_X%2C_p.41%29_-_Copy.jpg/220px-Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_%28April_1853%2C_X%2C_p.41%29_-_Copy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_%28April_1853%2C_X%2C_p.41%29_-_Copy.jpg/330px-Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_%28April_1853%2C_X%2C_p.41%29_-_Copy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_%28April_1853%2C_X%2C_p.41%29_-_Copy.jpg/440px-Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_%28April_1853%2C_X%2C_p.41%29_-_Copy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Part_of_the_Great_Wall_of_China_(April_1853,_X,_p.41)_-_Copy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of the Great Wall of China (April 1853, X, p. 41)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juvenile1853_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juvenile1853-38">[38]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greatwall_large.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1473" data-file-width="1275" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Greatwall_large.jpg/220px-Greatwall_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Greatwall_large.jpg/330px-Greatwall_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Greatwall_large.jpg/440px-Greatwall_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greatwall_large.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Wall in 1907</div></div></div>
<p>None of the <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans</a> who visited China or Mongolia in the 13th and 14th centuries, such as <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Pian_del_Carpine" title="Giovanni da Pian del Carpine">Giovanni da Pian del Carpine</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">William of Rubruck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odoric_of_Pordenone" title="Odoric of Pordenone">Odoric of Pordenone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_de%27_Marignolli" title="Giovanni de' Marignolli">Giovanni de' Marignolli</a>, mentioned the Great Wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaw200653–54_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaw200653–54-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The North African traveler <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a>, who also visited China during the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> c. 1346, had heard about China's Great Wall, possibly before he had arrived in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaw200654–55_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaw200654–55-41">[41]</a></sup> He wrote that the wall is "sixty days' travel" from Zeitun (modern <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a>) in his travelogue <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gift_to_Those_Who_Contemplate_the_Wonders_of_Cities_and_the_Marvels_of_Travelling" title="Gift to Those Who Contemplate the Wonders of Cities and the Marvels of Travelling">Gift to Those Who Contemplate the Wonders of Cities and the Marvels of Travelling</a></i>. He associated it with the <a href="/wiki/Gates_of_Alexander" title="Gates of Alexander">legend of the wall</a> mentioned in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhul-Qarnayn" title="Dhul-Qarnayn">Dhul-Qarnayn</a> (commonly associated with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>) was said to have erected to protect people near the land of the rising sun from the savages of <a href="/wiki/Gog_and_Magog" title="Gog and Magog">Gog and Magog</a>. However, Ibn Battuta could find no one who had either seen it or knew of anyone who had seen it, suggesting that although there were remnants of the wall at that time, they were not significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaw200653–55_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaw200653–55-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon after Europeans reached Ming China by ship in the early 16th century, accounts of the Great Wall started to circulate in Europe, even though no European was to see it for another century. Possibly one of the earliest European descriptions of the wall and of its significance for the defense of the country against the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tartars" title="Tartars">Tartars</a>" (i.e. Mongols) may be the one contained in <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_de_Barros" title="João de Barros">João de Barros</a>'s 1563 <i>Asia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Other early accounts in Western sources include those of <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_da_Cruz" title="Gaspar da Cruz">Gaspar da Cruz</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bento_de_Goes" title="Bento de Goes">Bento de Goes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a>, and Bishop <a href="/wiki/Juan_Gonz%C3%A1lez_de_Mendoza" title="Juan González de Mendoza">Juan González de Mendoza</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990204–05_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990204–05-45">[45]</a></sup> the latter in 1585 describing it as a "superbious and mightie work" of architecture, though he had not seen it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lach_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lach-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1559, in his work "A Treatise of China and the Adjoyning Regions", Gaspar da Cruz offers an early discussion of the Great Wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990204–05_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990204–05-45">[45]</a></sup> Perhaps the first recorded instance of a European actually entering China via the Great Wall came in 1605, when the Portuguese Jesuit brother <a href="/wiki/Bento_de_G%C3%B3is" title="Bento de Góis">Bento de Góis</a> reached the northwestern <a href="/wiki/Jiayu_Pass" title="Jiayu Pass">Jiayu Pass</a> from India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYule1866579_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYule1866579-47">[47]</a></sup> Early European accounts were mostly modest and empirical, closely mirroring contemporary Chinese understanding of the Wall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron19902–4_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron19902–4-48">[48]</a></sup> although later they slid into hyperbole,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990206_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990206-49">[49]</a></sup> including the erroneous but ubiquitous claim that the Ming walls were the same ones that were built by the first emperor in the 3rd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990206_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990206-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>When China opened its borders to foreign merchants and visitors after its defeat in the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium Wars</a>, the Great Wall became a main attraction for tourists. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travelogue_(literature)" title="Travelogue (literature)">travelogues</a> of the later 19th century further enhanced the reputation and the mythology of the Great Wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990209_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990209-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Inner_Great_Wall"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Course">Course</span></h2>
<p>A formal definition of what constitutes a "Great Wall" has not been agreed upon, making the full course of the Great Wall difficult to describe in its entirety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHessler200760_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHessler200760-51">[51]</a></sup> The defensive lines contain multiple stretches of ramparts, trenches and ditches, as well as individual fortresses.
</p><p>In 2012, based on existing research and the results of a comprehensive mapping survey, the <a href="/wiki/National_Cultural_Heritage_Administration" title="National Cultural Heritage Administration">National Cultural Heritage Administration</a> of China concluded that the remaining Great Wall associated sites include 10,051 wall sections, 1,764 ramparts or trenches, 29,510 individual buildings, and 2,211 fortifications or passes, with the walls and trenches spanning a total length of 21,196.18 km (13,170.70 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncha_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-ncha-3">[3]</a></sup> Incorporating advanced technologies, the study has concluded that the <a href="/wiki/Ming_Great_Wall" title="Ming Great Wall">Ming Great Wall</a> measures 8,850 km (5,500 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Wall_of_China_'even_longer'_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Wall_of_China_'even_longer'-52">[52]</a></sup> This consists of 6,259 km (3,889 mi) of wall sections, 359 km (223 mi) of trenches and 2,232 km (1,387 mi) of natural defensive barriers such as hills and rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Wall_of_China_'even_longer'_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Wall_of_China_'even_longer'-52">[52]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> and earlier Great Wall sites are 3,080 km (1,914 mi) long in total; <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty (1115–1234)</a> <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)#Jin_Great_Wall" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">border fortifications</a> are 4,010 km (2,492 mi) in length; the remainder date back to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Qi" title="Northern Qi">Northern Qi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties" title="Five Dynasties">Five Dynasties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xixia" title="Xixia">Xixia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncha_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-ncha-3">[3]</a></sup> About half of the sites are located in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> (31%) and <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a> (19%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncha_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-ncha-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Han_Great_Wall">Han Great Wall</span></h3>
<p>Han fortifications starts from <a href="/wiki/Yumen_Pass" title="Yumen Pass">Yumen Pass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yang_Pass" title="Yang Pass">Yang Pass</a>, southwest of <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> province. Ruins of the remotest Han border posts are found in Mamitu (<span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A6%AC" title="wikt:馬">馬</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%BF%B7%E9%80%94" title="wikt:迷途">迷途</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A9%AC" title="wikt:马">马</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%BF%B7%E9%80%94" title="wikt:迷途">迷途</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Mǎmítú</i>, <span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">l</a></small> "horses losing their way"</span>) near Yumen Pass.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ming_Great_Wall">Ming Great Wall</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jiayuguan_(pass)" title="Jiayuguan (pass)">Jiayu Pass</a>, located in Gansu province, is the western terminus of the Ming Great Wall. From Jiayu Pass the wall travels discontinuously down the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> and into the deserts of <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">Ningxia</a>, where it enters the western edge of the Yellow River loop at <a href="/wiki/Yinchuan" title="Yinchuan">Yinchuan</a>. Here the first major walls erected during the Ming dynasty cut through the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_Desert" title="Ordos Desert">Ordos Desert</a> to the eastern edge of the Yellow River loop. There at Piantou Pass <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%81%8F" title="wikt:偏">偏</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A0%AD" title="wikt:頭">頭</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%97%9C" title="wikt:關">關</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%81%8F" title="wikt:偏">偏</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%B4" title="wikt:头">头</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%85%B3" title="wikt:关">关</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Piāntóuguān</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Xinzhou" title="Xinzhou">Xinzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> province, the Great Wall splits in two with the "Outer Great Wall" <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%96" title="wikt:外">外</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%95%B7%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:長城">長城</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%96" title="wikt:外">外</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%95%BF%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:长城">长城</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Wài Chǎngchéng</i>) extending along the Inner Mongolia border with Shanxi into <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a> province, and the "Inner Great Wall" <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%85%A7" title="wikt:內">內</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%95%B7%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:長城">長城</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%85%A7" title="wikt:內">內</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%95%BF%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:长城">长城</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Nèi Chǎngchéng</i>) running southeast from Piantou Pass for some 400 km (250 mi), passing through important passes like the <a href="/wiki/Pingxing_Pass" title="Pingxing Pass">Pingxing Pass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yanmen_Pass" title="Yanmen Pass">Yanmen Pass</a> before joining the Outer Great Wall at Sihaiye <span class="nowrap">(<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9B%9B%E6%B5%B7" title="wikt:四海">四海</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%86%B6" title="wikt:冶">冶</a></span></span>, <i>Sìhǎiyě</i>),</span> in Beijing's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yanqing_County" title="Yanqing County">Yanqing County</a>.
</p><p>The sections of the Great Wall around Beijing municipality are especially famous: they were frequently renovated and are regularly visited by tourists today. The <a href="/wiki/Badaling" title="Badaling">Badaling</a> Great Wall near <a href="/wiki/Zhangjiakou" title="Zhangjiakou">Zhangjiakou</a> is the most famous stretch of the wall, for this was the first section to be opened to the public in the People's Republic of China, as well as the showpiece stretch for foreign dignitaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERojas2010140_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERojas2010140-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Badaling" title="Badaling">Badaling</a> Great Wall saw nearly 10 million visitors in 2018, and in 2019, a daily limit of 65,000 visitors was instated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> South of Badaling is the <a href="/wiki/Juyong_Pass" title="Juyong Pass">Juyong Pass</a>; when it was used by the Chinese to protect their land, this section of the wall had many guards to defend the capital Beijing. Made of stone and bricks from the hills, this portion of the Great Wall is 7.8 m (25 ft 7 in) high and 5 m (16 ft 5 in) wide.
</p><p>One of the most striking sections of the Ming Great Wall is where it climbs extremely steep slopes in <a href="/wiki/Jinshanling" title="Jinshanling">Jinshanling</a>. There it runs 11 km (7 mi) long, ranges from 5 to 8 m (16 ft 5 in to 26 ft 3 in) in height, and 6 m (19 ft 8 in) across the bottom, narrowing up to 5 m (16 ft 5 in) across the top. Wangjing Lou <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9C%9B" title="wikt:望">望</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BA%AC" title="wikt:京">京</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%A8%93" title="wikt:樓">樓</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9C%9B" title="wikt:望">望</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BA%AC" title="wikt:京">京</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%A5%BC" title="wikt:楼">楼</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Wàngjīng Lóu</i>) is one of Jinshanling's 67 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watchtowers" title="Watchtowers">watchtowers</a>, 980 m (3,220 ft) above sea level. Southeast of Jinshanling is the <a href="/wiki/Mutianyu" title="Mutianyu">Mutianyu</a> Great Wall which winds along lofty, cragged mountains from the southeast to the northwest for 2.25 km (1.40 mi). It is connected with Juyongguan Pass to the west and Gubeikou to the east. This section was one of the first to be renovated following the turmoil of the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindesay2008212_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindesay2008212-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the edge of the Bohai Gulf is Shanhai Pass, considered the traditional end of the Great Wall and the "First Pass <a href="/wiki/Tianxia" title="Tianxia">Under Heaven</a>". The part of the wall inside Shanhai Pass that meets the sea is named the "Old Dragon Head". 3 km (2 mi) north of Shanhai Pass is Jiaoshan Great Wall (<span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%84%A6%E5%B1%B1" title="wikt:焦山">焦山</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%95%B7%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:長城">長城</a>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%84%A6" title="wikt:焦">焦</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B1%B1" title="wikt:山">山</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%95%BF%E5%9F%8E" title="wikt:长城">长城</a>,</span> <i>Jiāoshān Chángchéng</i>), the site of the first mountain of the Great Wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> 15 km (9 mi) northeast from Shanhaiguan is <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E4%B9%9D%E9%97%A8%E5%8F%A3" title="zh:九门口">Jiumenkou</a> <span class="nowrap">(<small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" title="Traditional characters">t</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B9%9D" title="wikt:九">九</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%96%80" title="wikt:門">門</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%8F%A3" title="wikt:口">口</a></span></span>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">s</a></small> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B9%9D" title="wikt:九">九</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%97%A8" title="wikt:门">门</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%8F%A3" title="wikt:口">口</a></span></span>,</span> <i>Jiǔménkǒu</i>), which is the only portion of the wall that was built as a bridge.
</p><p>In 2009, 180 km of previously unknown sections of the Ming wall concealed by hills, trenches and rivers were discovered with the help of infrared range finders and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a> devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In March and April 2015, nine sections with a total length of more than 10 km (6 mi), believed to be part of the Great Wall, were discovered along the border of <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">Ningxia</a> autonomous region and Gansu province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<p>Before the use of bricks, the Great Wall was mainly built from rammed earth, stones, and wood. During the Ming, however, bricks were heavily used in many areas of the wall, as were materials such as tiles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lime_(mineral)" title="Lime (mineral)">lime</a>, and stone. The size and weight of the bricks made them easier to work with than earth and stone, so construction quickened. Additionally, bricks could bear more weight and endure better than rammed earth. Stone can hold under its own weight better than brick, but is more difficult to use. Consequently, stones cut into rectangular shapes were used for the foundation, inner and outer <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/brim" title="wikt:brim">brims</a>, and gateways of the wall. <a href="/wiki/Battlement" title="Battlement">Battlements</a> line the uppermost portion of the vast majority of the wall, with defensive gaps a little over 30 cm (12 in) tall, and about 23 cm (9.1 in) wide. From the parapets, guards could survey the surrounding land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurnbull200729_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurnbull200729-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sticky_rice_mortar" title="Sticky rice mortar">Sticky rice mortar</a>, consisting of sticky rice soup mixed with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">slaked lime</a>, was extensively used to hold bricks together;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> no human <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a> or body parts were ever incorporated into the mortar or any part of the wall, contrary to what a legend states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Communication between the army units along the length of the Great Wall, including the ability to call reinforcements and warn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garrisons" title="Garrisons">garrisons</a> of enemy movements, was of high importance. Signal towers were built upon hill tops or other high points along the wall for their visibility. Wooden gates could be used as a trap against those going through. Barracks, stables, and armories were built near the wall's inner surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurnbull200729_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurnbull200729-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Condition">Condition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg/220px-A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg/330px-A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg/440px-A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_long_stretch_of_the_Great_Wall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A more rural portion of the Great Wall that stretches through the mountains, here seen in slight disrepair</div></div></div>
<p>While portions north of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> and near tourist centers have been preserved and even extensively renovated, in many other locations the wall is in disrepair. The wall sometimes provided a source of stones to build houses and roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Sections of the wall are also prone to <a href="/wiki/Graffiti" title="Graffiti">graffiti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vandalism" title="Vandalism">vandalism</a>, while inscribed bricks were pilfered and sold on the market for up to 50 <a href="/wiki/Renminbi" title="Renminbi">renminbi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30percent_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-30percent-65">[65]</a></sup> Parts have been destroyed to make way for construction or mining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2012 report by the <a href="/wiki/National_Cultural_Heritage_Administration" title="National Cultural Heritage Administration">National Cultural Heritage Administration</a> states that 22% of the Ming Great Wall has disappeared, while 1,961 km (1,219 mi) of wall have vanished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30percent_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-30percent-65">[65]</a></sup> More than 60 km (37 mi) of the wall in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> province may disappear in the next 20 years, due to erosion from <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">sandstorms</a>. In some places, the height of the wall has been reduced from more than 5 m (16 ft 5 in) to less than 2 m (6 ft 7 in). Various square lookout towers that characterize the most famous images of the wall have disappeared. Many western sections of the wall are <a href="/wiki/Mudbrick" title="Mudbrick">constructed from mud</a>, rather than brick and stone, and thus are more susceptible to erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 2014 a portion of the wall near the border of Liaoning and Hebei province was repaired with concrete. The work has been much criticized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visibility_from_space">Visibility from space</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_the_Moon">From the Moon</span></h3>
<p>The Great Wall of China cannot be seen from the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Even though the myth is thoroughly debunked,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debunked_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Debunked-70">[70]</a></sup> it is still ingrained in popular culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Times-71">[71]</a></sup> The apparent width of the Great Wall from the Moon would be the same as that of a human hair viewed from 3 km (2 mi) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELópez-Gil20083–4_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELópez-Gil20083–4-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the earliest known references to the myth that the Great Wall can be seen from the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> appears in a letter written in 1754 by the English antiquary <a href="/wiki/William_Stukeley" title="William Stukeley">William Stukeley</a>. Stukeley wrote that, "This mighty wall [<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_wall" title="Hadrian's wall">Hadrian's wall</a>] of four score miles [130 km] in length is only exceeded by the Chinese Wall, which makes a considerable figure upon the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrestrial_globe" title="Terrestrial globe">terrestrial globe</a>, and may be discerned at the Moon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The claim was also mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Norman,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Henry Norman, 1st Baronet">Henry Norman</a> in 1895 where he states "besides its age it enjoys the reputation of being the only work of human hands on the globe visible from the Moon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The issue of <a href="/wiki/Martian_canals" title="Martian canals">"canals" on Mars</a> was prominent in the late 19th century and may have led to the belief that long, thin objects were visible from space. The claim that the Great Wall is visible from the moon also appears in 1932's <i><a href="/wiki/Ripley%27s_Believe_It_or_Not!" title="Ripley's Believe It or Not!">Ripley's Believe It or Not!</a></i> strip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ripley_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ripley-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_low_Earth_orbit">From low Earth orbit</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Wall_of_China,_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="739" data-file-width="1638" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Great_Wall_of_China%2C_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg/220px-Great_Wall_of_China%2C_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Great_Wall_of_China%2C_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg/330px-Great_Wall_of_China%2C_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Great_Wall_of_China%2C_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg/440px-Great_Wall_of_China%2C_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Wall_of_China,_Satellite_image_red_composite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Identical satellite images of a section of the Great Wall in northern Shanxi, running diagonally from lower left to upper right and not to be confused with the more prominent river running from upper left to lower right. In the image on the right, the Great Wall has been outlined in red. The region pictured is 12 km × 12 km (7 mi × 7 mi).</div></div></div>
<p>A more controversial question is whether the wall is visible from <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (an altitude of as little as 160 km (100 mi)). <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> claims that it is barely visible, and only under nearly perfect conditions; it is no more conspicuous than many other human-made objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Veteran US astronaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Cernan" title="Eugene Cernan">Gene Cernan</a> has stated: "At Earth orbit of 100 to 200 miles [160 to 320 km] high, the Great Wall of China is, indeed, visible to the naked eye." <a href="/wiki/Ed_Lu" title="Ed Lu">Ed Lu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Expedition_7" title="Expedition 7">Expedition 7</a> Science Officer aboard the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, adds that, "It's less visible than a lot of other objects. And you have to know where to look."
</p><p>In October 2003, Chinese astronaut <a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a> stated that he had not been able to see the Great Wall of China. In response, the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) issued a press release reporting that from an orbit between 160 and 320 km (100 and 200 mi), the Great Wall is visible to the naked eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELópez-Gil20083–4_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELópez-Gil20083–4-72">[72]</a></sup> The image was actually a river in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leroy_Chiao" title="Leroy Chiao">Leroy Chiao</a>, a Chinese-American astronaut, took a photograph from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> that shows the wall. It was so indistinct that the photographer was not certain he had actually captured it. Based on the photograph, the <i><a href="/wiki/China_Daily" title="China Daily">China Daily</a></i> later reported that the Great Wall can be seen from 'space' with the naked eye, under favorable viewing conditions, if one knows exactly where to look.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELópez-Gil20083–4_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELópez-Gil20083–4-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-nyt-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-nyt_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFThe_New_York_Times_with_introduction_by_Sam_Tanenhaus2011"><i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> with introduction by <a href="/wiki/Sam_Tanenhaus" title="Sam Tanenhaus">Sam Tanenhaus</a> (2011). <i>The New York Times Guide to Essential Knowledge: A Desk Reference for the Curious Mind</i>. <a href="/wiki/St._Martin%27s_Press" title="St. Martin's Press">St. Martin's Press</a> of <a href="/wiki/Macmillan_Publishers" title="Macmillan Publishers">Macmillan Publishers</a>. p. 1131. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-312-64302-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-312-64302-7"><bdi>978-0-312-64302-7</bdi></a>. <q>Beginning as separate sections of fortification around the 7th century B.C.E and unified during the Qin Dynasty in the 3rd century B.C.E, this wall, built of earth and rubble with a facing of brick or stone, runs from east to west across China for over 4,000 miles.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+New+York+Times+Guide+to+Essential+Knowledge%3A+A+Desk+Reference+for+the+Curious+Mind&amp;rft.pages=1131&amp;rft.pub=St.+Martin%27s+Press+of+Macmillan+Publishers&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-312-64302-7&amp;rft.au=%27%27The+New+York+Times%27%27+with+introduction+by+Sam+Tanenhaus&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreat+Wall+of+China"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Brit-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Brit_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/243863/Great-Wall-of-China" rel="nofollow">"Great Wall of China"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i>. <q>Large parts of the fortification system date from the 7th through the 4th century BC. In the 3rd century BC Shihuangdi (Qin Shi Huang), the first emperor of a united China (under the Qin dynasty), connected a number of existing defensive walls into a single system. Traditionally, the eastern terminus of the wall was considered to be <a href="/wiki/Shanhai_Pass" title="Shanhai Pass">Shanhai Pass</a> (Shanhaiguan) on the coast of the Bohai (Gulf of Zhili), and the wall's length—without its branches and other secondary sections—was thought to extend for some 6,690 km (4,160 mi).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Great+Wall+of+China&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclop%C3%A6dia+Britannica&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2FEBchecked%2Ftopic%2F243863%2FGreat-Wall-of-China&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreat+Wall+of+China"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990202-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron1990202_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWaldron1990">Waldron 1990</a>, p. 202.
